    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="9ccc8623a22f4340bf5fd41885224d63">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '6241e31c-ce34-41c5-873a-928952bb0dca'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d65608208" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Iron sulfide (FeS) has been extensively studied as sodium-ion battery anodes due to its high theoretical capacity (609 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), but its large volume expansion and low electrical conductivity result in unsatisfactory cycling life and poor rate performance. Moreover, the sodium ion storage mechanism of FeS at a voltage range of 0.01–1&nbsp;V involving conversion reactions and subsequent ion storage process is unclear yet. Here, the study proposes a vapor-pressure induced synthesis route to fabricate FeS/C yolk-shell structure that ultrathin carbon layers coat on the surface of FeS nanosheets, which can accommodate volume expansion of FeS during sodiation observed via in situ transmission electron microscope and improve its electrical conductivity. Remarkably, an in situ magnetometry reveals that vast spin-polarized electrons can be injected into superparamagnetic Fe nanoparticles (≈3&nbsp;nm) formed during conversion reaction to induce evolution of electrode magnetization between 0.01 and 1&nbsp;V, during which spin-polarized surface capacitance effect occurs at Fe/Na<sub>2</sub>S interfaces to increase extra ion storage and boost ion transport stably. Consequently, the FeS/C yolk-shell nanosheets deliver a high reversible capacity of 664.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and 300.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 10&nbsp;000 cycles at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 81.1%.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400246-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400246-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been widely used in portable electronic devices, electric vehicles (EVs), and large-scale energy storage systems (LSESS) due to their high energy density, along with long cycling life and good fast-charging performances.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0001_R_d65608047e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the scarcity of lithium reserves on the earth makes LIBs unable to meet the requirement of rapid development of EVs and LSESS in future decades.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0002_R_d65608047e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, it is urgent to explore a rechargeable battery as a supplement or substitute for LIBs. Among rechargeable batteries, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as the most promising supplement or substitute of LIBs due to abundant sodium reserves in the earth's crust and their similar ion storage mechanism with LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0003_R_d65608047e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Energy">3</a>-<a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0005_R_d65608047e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, the large radius (0.102&nbsp;nm) of sodium ions and the fact that the commercial graphite anode of LIBs is unsuitable for SIBs have seriously hindered the commercialization progress of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0006_R_d65608047e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Environ. Sci.">6</a>-<a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0008_R_d65608047e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Commun.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although hard carbon is studied as a first-generation SIB anode due to its low price, its low capacity, poor rate performance, unsatisfactory cycling stability, and safety issues caused by unsatisfactory solid-electrolyte-interphase formation make it possess significant challenges in the application of high-speed EVs and LSESS.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0009_R_d65608047e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore,there is an urgent need to explore high-performance SIB anode materials.</p>
         
         <p>Transition metal sulfides (FeS, CuS, WS<sub>2</sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub>, etc),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0010_R_d65608047e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Eng. J.">10</a>-<a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0014_R_d65608047e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Sci.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> transition metal oxides (TiO<sub>2</sub>, Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, etc),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0015_R_d65608047e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Rare Metals">15</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0016_R_d65608047e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Sci.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> silicon,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0017_R_d65608047e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Rare Metals">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and phosphorus<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0018_R_d65608047e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have attained wide attention. Among them, FeS, an important member of transition metal sulfides, has been widely researched due to its high theoretical capacity (609 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), high safety, and abundant reserves.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0010_R_d65608047e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Eng. J.">10</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0011_R_d65608047e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, large volume expansion during sodiation and low electrical conductivity of FeS result in its unstable cycling performance and poor rate capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0019_R_d65608047e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Energy Chem.">19</a>-<a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0021_R_d65608047e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Eng. J.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To solve the two problems, vast strategies have been proposed, in which decreasing size of FeS to nanoscale, synthesizing composite of FeS with carbon materials, and constructing porous or hollow nanostructures into FeS are regarded to be the most helpful,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0019_R_d65608047e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Energy Chem.">19</a>-<a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0022_R_d65608047e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Chem. Eng. J.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the corresponding reasons are as follows: 1) Nanostructure is favorable for accommodating large volume expansion without obvious pulverization and shortening diffuse distance of charge to enhance reaction kinetics, thus resulting in the improvement of cyclability and rate capability; 2) Carbon materials can inhibit the volume expansion of FeS and improve its electrical conductivity, thus enhancing the cycling performance and rate capability; 3) Porous or hollow structure can supply space to accommodate the volume expansion of FeS, thus improving cycling life. Consequently, the composites prepared simultaneously involving the above three strategies will inevitably achieve good electrochemical performances. For example, Li et&nbsp;al. synthesized a porous FeS/CFs nanocomposite that FeS nanoparticles were distributed into porous carbon nanofibers through wet-spinning and heat-temperature annealing, which exhibited a capacity of 438 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, 247 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and 283 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 400 cycles with ≈90% capacity retention.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0023_R_d65608047e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Xie et&nbsp;al. fabricated a FeS/NC nanocomposite that FeS nanoparticles were decorated on a 3D porous N, S co-doped carbon network via chemical blowing and high-temperature vulcanization route, which displayed a capacity of 520 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, 191 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and 387 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> with capacity retention of 78%.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0024_R_d65608047e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Huang et&nbsp;al. prepared a FeS/C nanocomposite that the FeS nanocrystals were encapsulated by in situ-generated bubble film-like porous carbon framework, which exhibited a capacity of 455 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, 219 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and 354 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1500 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> with capacity retention of 98%.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0025_R_d65608047e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Sci. China Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> An effective combination of the three strategies can indeed greatly improve cycling stability under a high current density of FeS. However, the above synthesis methods and electrochemical performances of corresponding obtained materials still have the following drawbacks in practical applications: 1) The obtained capacities (438–520 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) are low relative to theoretical capacity (609 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) due to excess carbon addition (20.1–53.8 wt.%), which will inevitably reduce energy density to impede application progress of FeS in the field of EVs; 2) The capacity at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> only retains 36.7–56.4% of that obtained at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which needs be improved further to meet the usage requirements of high-speed EVs; 3) The displayed cycling life (400–1500 cycles) is short, which is unable to meet the need (at least 5000 cycles) of LSESS; 4) These synthesis methods involve complex process (at least five steps) resulting in a long preparation time (at least 24&nbsp;h) to increase preparation cost and toxic raw materials, which are very harmful for health. As a result, exploring a simple, fast, and green route to fabricate high-specific-capacity, long-term-cycling, and outstanding-rate-capability FeS/C nanocomposites with hollow or porous structure is crucial to advance SIBs in the application progress of high-speed EVs and LSESS.</p>
         
         <p>As is well known to us, the sodium storage capacity of FeS comes from intercalation reaction (xNa<sup>+</sup> + xe<sup>−</sup> + FeS→Na<sub>x</sub>FeS) and conversion reaction ((2-x)Na<sup>+</sup>+(2-x)e<sup>−</sup> + Na<sub>x</sub>FeS→Fe + Na<sub>2</sub>S),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0024_R_d65608047e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and its rate performance also depends on whether the two reactions can be carried out quickly. Previous studies only explored the fast sodium storage mechanism of FeS in the intercalation reaction using density functional theory calculations.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0026_R_d65608047e632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Chem. Eng. J.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, as the dominant sodium storage process, the fast sodium storage mechanism in the conversion reaction has not yet been revealed. In addition, previous studies have demonstrated through in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) and cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves that the conversion reaction will be completed when the discharge voltage reaches ≈0.5&nbsp;V,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0019_R_d65608047e638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Energy Chem.">19</a>-<a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0025_R_d65608047e638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Sci. China Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> at which the FeS will be converted completely into Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>S. The discharge curves between 0.5 and 0.01&nbsp;V show that the FeS-based electrodes still can store sodium ions, and the sodium storage capacity accounts for ≈25% of the total capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0019_R_d65608047e645" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Energy Chem.">19</a>-<a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0025_R_d65608047e645" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Sci. China Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The origin of this part of capacity is not yet known, and in the voltage range the fast transport mechanism of sodium ions has not been studied and revealed. Therefore, it is crucial to reveal the storage mechanism of sodium ions of FeS-based electrodes within the voltage range, which will fill the research gap in the FeS sodium storage mechanism and lay a theoretical foundation for further improving the sodium storage performance of FeS. Unfortunately, there have been no research reports so far.</p>
         
         <p>Here, aiming at the problems mentioned above, we propose a one-step vapor-pressure induced synthesis route to fabricate a FeS/C yolk-shell composite nanosheets that ultrathin carbon layers coat on the surface of FeS nanosheets with a hollow space between the two. The structure has the following advantages: 1) The nanosheet structure can shorten ion transport distance to improve reaction kinetics and relieve volume expansion, thus ameliorating cycling life and rate capability; 2) The hollow space and carbon coating layers can accommodate the volume expansion of FeS during sodiation, thus improving cyclability; 3) The carbon coating layers can enhance the electrical conductivity of FeS, thus enhancing rate performance. Remarkably, we in-depthly clarify the sodium ion transport and storage mechanism of FeS/C electrode at a voltage range of 0.01–1&nbsp;V involving conversion reactions and subsequent ion storage process from the perspective of spin-polarized electrons through in situ magnetometry, in situ transmission electron microscope (TEM), ex situ TEM, and ex situ XRD. Specifically, the superparamagnetic Fe nanoparticles (≈3&nbsp;nm) can be formed during the conversion reaction of FeS to induce the evolution of electrode magnetization between 0.01 and 1&nbsp;V, which causes spin-polarized surface capacitance effect at Fe/Na<sub>2</sub>S interfaces to store extra ion and boost ion transport stably, especially at high current density. As a result, the FeS/C nanosheets deliver a high reversible capacity of 664.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 300.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 10&nbsp;000 cycles at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 81.1% in half cells. Furthermore, the full cells using Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as cathode can deliver a high energy density of 181.9&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which remains 70.5% at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and a high capacity retention of 71.9% after 1000 cycles at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The above performances are superior to the previous reports on FeS-carbon composite SIB anodes in half/full cells.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400246-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400246-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202400246-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202400246-sec-0030-title">2.1 Synthesis and Characterizations of Materials</h3>
            
            <p>The samples were fabricated in a homemade sealed vessel (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) with fixed 1.8&nbsp;ml precursors consisting of different volume ratios of iron naphthenate (INT, Fe(C<sub>11</sub>H<sub>7</sub>O<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub>) and mercaptoethanol (MCTE, HS(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub>OH) and the corresponding formation diagram of samples are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0001">1</a>. The INT can be thermally decomposed into vast carbon-containing gaseous groups and iron-containing oxygen groups, leading to an increase in pressure of the sealed vessel (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0001">1a</a>). Under high vapor-phase pressure, the iron-containing oxygen groups can act as Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nucleation points and grow into nanosheet structures. As the temperature continues to rise, the vapor-phase pressure increases accordingly based on the ideal gas law (PV = nRT), thus resulting in the transformation of carbon-containing gaseous groups into solid carbon to coat on the surface of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanosheets (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0001">1a</a>). Consequently, when only adding INT (ratio of 1:0), the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/C core-shell nanosheet structure with sub-micro size in the plane (300–500&nbsp;nm, Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a–d</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) is obtained. The pyrolysis of MCTE can produce vast sulfur-containing and carbon-containing gaseous groups, among which the sulfur-containing gaseous groups will sulfide the formed Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> to lead to the formation of iron sulfides. When the ratio is 1:0.5, only Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/C yolk-shell nanosheets with several micro sizes in the plane (2–10&nbsp;µm, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0001">1b</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2e–h</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) are formed due to the small amount of MCTE. When the ratio increases to 1:1, the FeS/C yolk-shell nanosheets with several micro sizes in the plane (2–10&nbsp;µm, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0001">1c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2i–l</a>, Supporting Information; <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0002">2a–c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) are obtained due to the proper amount of MCTE, and further increasing the MCTE amount (ratio of 1:2) will form carbon spheres (CS) besides FeS/C core-shell nanosheet (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0001">1d</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2m–p</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), and the corresponding sample is labeled as FeS/C@CS. For the FeS/C sample, the FeS nanosheets are coated by carbon nanolayers (a thickness size of ≈15.5&nbsp;nm) with a hollow space with a width of ≈16.2&nbsp;nm between the two (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0002">2a–d</a>), thus proving the formation of yolk-shell structure, which can accommodate the volume expansion of FeS during cycling to improve the cycling stability. The formation of the hollow structure may be attributed to the shrinkage of the initially formed nanosheets during subsequent high-temperature and vulcanization processes. The nanosheets show a lattice spacing of 0.298&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0002">2e</a>), corresponding to the (110) crystal plane of FeS to confirm the formation of FeS (JCPDS: 37–0477). The selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0002">2f</a>) shows a series of diffraction spots to confirm the single-crystal structure of the obtained FeS nanosheets. Furthermore, the diffraction spots are attributed to (110), (112), (114), (211), (300), and (008) crystal planes of FeS (JCPDS: 37–0477), further confirming that the nanosheets are FeS. The high-angle annular dark field (HAADF) image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0002">2g</a>) and corresponding energy diffraction spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mapping images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0002">2h–j</a>) show homogeneous distribution of Fe, S, and C elements in the FeS/C, proving that carbon nanolayers uniformly coat on the surface of FeS nanosheets. It should be noted that the hollow space is not tolerant of high-dose electron irradiation and shrinks during TEM observation (16.2&nbsp;nm in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0002">2c</a> and 9.5&nbsp;nm in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0002">2d</a>), making the carbon layers seem continuous in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0002">2j</a>. To further demonstrate the existence of the hollow space, a low-dose TEM observation is performed (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). A hollow space appears in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a,b</a> (Supporting Information) and a dark area appears between FeS and carbon layers in the HAADF image (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5c</a>, Supporting Information). The EDS line scan results show that the dark area and FeS possess similar C element intensity, indicating their similar thickness of carbon layers. Differently, the outside carbon shell shows a higher intensity of carbon elements. The results further prove the existence of a hollow space between carbon layers and FeS. Relative to INT with large molecular weight, the MCTE with small molecular weight after pyrolysis can produce higher vapor pressure in a sealed vessel under the same mass and temperature (detailed discussion shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information) to promote the conversion of containing-carbon gaseous radicals into CS. Specifically, the formation of CS in the FeS/C@CS sample is ascribed to the excess addition of MCTE, which would lead to the formation of more containing-carbon gaseous radicals in the sealed vessel. A portion of containing-carbon gaseous groups would transform into the carbon layers to coat the surface of FeS. Surplus carbon-containing gaseous groups start to nucleate and grow under high vapor-phase pressure. Carbon, a 2D material, is more likely to form sheet-like structures. However, under the isostatic vapor pressure, once sheet-like protrusions appear during nucleation and growth, they will bend and surround the previously formed nucleates or particles to seek support under the action of isostatic pressure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0027_R_d65608047e821" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Diam. Relat. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Because the sphere is the most mechanically stable structure, the CS is formed resultantly. Clearly, the size of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> obtained by INT as a solo precursor is much lower than those of iron sulfides obtained by INT and MCTE as precursors, which may be ascribed to the homogeneous nucleation of the initial&nbsp;formed Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> when INT&nbsp;as&nbsp;the&nbsp;solo&nbsp;precursor, thus increasing nucleation sites. When INT and MCTE are combined as precursors, the initially formed Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> undergoes heterogeneous nucleation due to the addition of MCTE, thus reducing nucleation sites. In a fixed reaction space, the increased nucleation sites would result in forming smaller particle sizes. Therefore, adding MCTE would increase the size of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, leading to an increase in the formed iron sulfides after vulcanization. In addition, an increase in MCTE leads to a rise in vapor-phase pressure (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), which may also promote the growth of iron sulfides, resulting in an increase in their size. Clearly, a hollow space appears in the Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4c</a>, Supporting Information) and FeS/C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0002">2c</a>) samples, while it does not appear between FeS and carbon layers in the FeS/C@CS sample (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6c</a>, Supporting Information) due to excess addition of MCTE. This would produce higher vapor pressure to promote the entrance of more containing-carbon gaseous radicals into the shrinkage space of the initially formed FeS nanosheets and their conversion into carbon layers, thus causing the absence of hollow space.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400246-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b4ae7983-a852-4759-8c6c-90a66ecd9568/aenm202400246-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b4ae7983-a852-4759-8c6c-90a66ecd9568/aenm202400246-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/790f2386-b881-49bd-a82e-c318077e6292/aenm202400246-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b4ae7983-a852-4759-8c6c-90a66ecd9568/aenm202400246-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400246-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400246" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic diagrams of the formation of samples: a) Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/C, b) Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/C, c) FeS/C, and d) FeS/C@CS.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400246-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a5ed4311-4abf-430a-a28c-fd55d2a3e1a5/aenm202400246-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a5ed4311-4abf-430a-a28c-fd55d2a3e1a5/aenm202400246-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1a422d0e-eeff-4441-902e-b5583987e25d/aenm202400246-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a5ed4311-4abf-430a-a28c-fd55d2a3e1a5/aenm202400246-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400246-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400246" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TEM characterizations of FeS/C: a–e) TEM images, f) SAED pattern, and g) HAADF and h–j) corresponding EDS mapping images of FeS/C. Structural and composition characterizations of the four samples: k) XRD patterns, l) Raman spectra, and m) TGA curves.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Then, the crystalline structures of the four samples are further characterized by the XRD pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0002">2j</a>). The Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/C shows diffraction peaks of (220), (311), (400), (422), (511), and (440) crystal planes of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (JCPDS: 19–0629), further indicating the formation of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. The Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/C shows diffraction peaks of (200), (206), (2012), (220), and (413) crystal planes of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S (JCPDS: 29–0725), further confirming the existence of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S. The FeS/C and FeS/C@CS show diffraction peaks of (110), (112), (201), (114), (211), (300), (008), and (224) crystal planes of FeS (JCPDS: 37–0477), further proving the existence of FeS in the two samples. Moreover, in the Raman spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0002">2l</a>), the four samples exhibit two peaks of carbon materials at ≈1345 (D band) and 1595 (G band) cm<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating the existence of carbon. The intensity ratios of D and G peaks (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub><i>/I</i><sub>G</sub> ratios) can indicate the disorder degree of carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0028_R_d65608047e940" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">28</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0029_R_d65608047e940" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nano-Micro Lett">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub><i>/I</i><sub>G</sub> ratios of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/C, Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/C, FeS/C, and FeS/C@CS are 0.84, 0.74, 0.67, and 0.44, respectively. The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub><i>/I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio represents a decreased trend with the increased amount of MCTE. As discussed above, the vapor pressure value caused by pyrolysis of MCTE is higher than INT. Higher vapor pressure may boost the improvement of the crystallinity of carbon materials, thus obtaining a lower <i>I</i><sub>D</sub><i>/I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio. It should be noted that the Raman spectrum in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0002">2l</a> of FeS/C@CS sample is collected from the FeS/C nanosheet, and the Raman spectrum of CS is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a> (Supporting Information). Obviously, the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio of CS is higher than FeS/C due to its amorphous characteristic (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6i</a>, Supporting Information). The higher crystallinity of the carbon layer coated on the surface of FeS than CS may be ascribed to the following two reasons. First, the initially formed Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and/or its vulcanization product (FeS) may have a catalytic effect on the coated carbon layer to improve its crystallinity. Second, the vapor-phase pressure during the formation of the carbon layer may be higher than that of the formation of CS. Higher vapor-phase pressure can promote the improvement of crystallinity of carbon. The electrical conductivity of composite powder is shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), and it is related to the electrical conductivity of its constituent materials and the number of interfaces between composite powder particles. Generally, materials with higher electrical conductivity yield composite materials with higher electrical conductivity, and fewer interparticle interfaces result in higher electrical conductivity. For our composite powder, the intrinsic electrical conductivity of iron sulfides is higher than that of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. The electrical conductivity of carbon is related to its content and crystallinity. The higher the content and the better the crystallinity, the higher the electrical conductivity. The number of interfaces between particles is related to their size, and a smaller size will lead to more contact interfaces between particles, thereby reducing electrical conductivity. Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/C has the highest carbon content, but Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> has a low intrinsic electrical conductivity, low carbon crystallinity, and small particle size causing vast interfaces, thus resulting in its lowest electrical conductivity (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). For Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/C, FeS/C, and FeS/C@CS, their particle sizes are similar. Thus, the crystallinity and content of carbon play a decisive role in electrical conductivity. Besides, the electrical conductivity is more correlated with the carbon content than the crystallinity of carbon. Consequently, as the carbon content increases, the electrical conductivity shows an increasing trend for the three samples (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Besides, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) survey spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a>, Supporting Information) further confirm the Fe, O, and C elements in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/C and Fe, S, O, and C elements in other three samples. The O existence in the Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/C, FeS/C, and FeS/C@CS may be ascribed to adventitious impurities from the environment, such as adsorption of surface oxygen or carbon dioxide.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0030_R_d65608047e1025" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Mater. Chem. A">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The corresponding atomic percentages of each element calculated based on the XPS survey spectra are shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), which further confirms the formation of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S (x = 0.15), and FeS. All the Fe 2p XPS spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8c</a>, Supporting Information) show two peaks ≈724.8 (Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub>) and 711.3 (Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub>) eV, in which binding energies for Fe<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Fe<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> are higher than Fe<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0026_R_d65608047e1065" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Chem. Eng. J.">26</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0031_R_d65608047e1065" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0032_R_d65608047e1065" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/C sample, the dominant valence state of Fe is +3, while the dominant valence state of Fe is +2 for other three samples. Thus, the binding energy of Fe 2p of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/C is higher than other three samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8c</a>, Supporting Information). All the S 2p XPS spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8d</a>, Supporting Information) show two peaks ≈162.4 and 161.3&nbsp;eV ascribed to S<sup>2−</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and S<sup>2−</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0026_R_d65608047e1094" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Chem. Eng. J.">26</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0031_R_d65608047e1094" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0032_R_d65608047e1094" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides the two peaks, similar with the previous reports,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0026_R_d65608047e1100" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Chem. Eng. J.">26</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0031_R_d65608047e1100" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a>-<a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0033_R_d65608047e1100" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Rare Met">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/C also shows two obvious peaks at 163.6 and 167.0&nbsp;eV ascribed to the S<sub>n</sub><sup>2−</sup> and SO<sub>x</sub>, respectively, as shown in the fitted S 2p spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). All the C 1s spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8e</a>, Supporting Information) show a peak ≈284.3&nbsp;eV ascribed to C-C bonds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0034_R_d65608047e1119" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> further confirming the existence of carbon materials. The binding energy represents a decreased trend with the increase of addition amount of MCTE, which should be attributed to the enhancement of the crystalline degree of carbon, as confirmed in Raman spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0002">2l</a>). The higher crystalline degree of carbon would result in a lower binding energies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0035_R_d65608047e1128" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">35</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0036_R_d65608047e1128" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Energy Stor.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To determine the content of carbon materials in the four samples, thermal gravimetric analysis (TGA) is performed in the air (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0002">2m</a>), during which the samples are heated to 1000&nbsp;°C at a ramp rate of 10&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup>. After high-temperature oxidation, the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S, and FeS can be converted into Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, while the carbon can be converted into CO<sub>2</sub>. Thus, the final residue is Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> for all the samples. The conversion of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> into Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> can result in a mass increase of 3.5%. The conversion of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S (x = 0.15) and FeS into Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> can cause a mass decrease of 10.3 and 9.1%, respectively. The TGA results show that the mass percentages of residues for Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/C, Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/C, FeS/C, and FeS/C@CS are 63.2, 80.1, 76.3, and 67.6%, respectively. Based on the results, the carbon content of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/C, Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/C, FeS/C, and FeS/C@CS can be calculated as 38.9, 10.7, 16.1, and 25.6&nbsp;wt.%, respectively. For the samples containing Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S and FeS, the initial mass increase below 360&nbsp;°C is ascribed to partial chemical reaction of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/FeS into FeSO<sub>4</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0034_R_d65608047e1190" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0037_R_d65608047e1190" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nano Res">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With the increase of temperature to 390&nbsp;°C, the conversion of remaining Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/FeS and FeSO<sub>4</sub> into Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> causes the mass decrease.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0034_R_d65608047e1204" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0037_R_d65608047e1204" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nano Res">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Then, a significant mass loss occurs during 530–680&nbsp;°C, mainly attributed to carbon material oxidation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0034_R_d65608047e1210" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/C possesses the highest carbon content, which is ascribed to its highest specific surface area (69.9 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8f</a>, Supporting Information). Higher specific surface area can result in more carbon layers coating, thus increasing the carbon content. Compared with Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/C sample, the specific surface areas of the formed Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/C, FeS/C, and FeS/C@CS rapidly decrease (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8f</a>, Supporting Information) due to their lower carbon content than Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/C, and larger size (several micro sizes in plane size) than Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/C (sub-micro sizes in plane size). The lower carbon content can increase the density of composites due to the far lower density of carbon materials than Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S, and FeS, thus decreasing the specific surface area. Obviously, a larger size can also decrease the specific surface area. The lowest specific surface area of FeS/C is favorable for reducing side reactions between materials and electrolytes to enhance the first Coulombic efficiency (CE).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202400246-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202400246-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance Characterizations in Half Cells</h3>
            
            <p>The capacities obtained in half cells are calculated based on the total mass of active materials, including FeS and C shells. The first charge/discharge curves (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0003">3a</a>) show that Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/C, Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/C, FeS/C, and FeS/C@CS have first charge/discharge capacities of 450.8/793.7, 614.8/794.8, 664.9/815.6, and 550.3/756.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to the first CE of 56.8, 77.4, 81.5, and 72.8%, respectively. The highest first CE of FeS/C is ascribed to its lowest specific surface area (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7e</a>, Supporting Information). The first discharge capacity loss primarily arises from formation of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0030_R_d65608047e1278" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Mater. Chem. A">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/C and FeS/C exhibit higher capacities than Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/C and FeS/C@CS, which is caused by their relatively lower carbon content (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0002">2m</a>) due to lower sodium-ion storage capacity of carbon materials than Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S, or FeS. The FeS/C with slightly higher carbon content shows a higher capacity than Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/C, which is ascribed to 1) higher theoretical sodium-ion storage capacity of FeS than Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S and 2) higher electrical conductivity of FeS/C than Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/C (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), which is favorable for more fully sodiation of FeS to increase capacity. After 100 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0003">3b</a>), the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/C, Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/C, FeS/C, and FeS/C@CS show capacities of 358.6, 579.3, 636.9, and 512.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to the capacity retentions of 79.5, 94.2, 95.8, and 93.2%, respectively. Besides, the FeS/C shows more excellent rate performances with capacities of 665.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, 420.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and 328.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 A g<sup>−1</sup> than other samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0003">3c</a>). Clearly, the capacity at 20 A g<sup>−1</sup> remains 49.4% of the capacity at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, fully demonstrating superior rate capability of FeS/C. The best rate capability of FeS/C arises from i) its lowest charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>, semicircle diameter of high-frequency zone) and the fastest ion diffusion capability (the highest straight-line slope at low-frequency zone), as tested in electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0003">3d</a>); ii) its highest capacitive contribution, confirmed by testing CV curves at different scanning rates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0003">3e–g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). The peak current (i) and the scan rate (v) in the CV curves obeys the equation that <i>i</i>  =  <i>av<sup>b</sup></i>, in which b-values of 0.5 and 1 are ascribed to diffusion-controlled behavior and capacitive-controlled process, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0029_R_d65608047e1361" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nano-Micro Lett">29</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0034_R_d65608047e1361" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0038_R_d65608047e1361" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Rare Met">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The b values of FeS/C electrodes for peaks 1 and 2 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0003">3e</a>) are 0.83 and 0.94 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0003">3f</a>), respectively, suggesting that the sodium-ion storage dynamics of the FeS/C electrode involve diffusion-controlled and capacitive-controlled mechanisms, in which the capacitive contribution is dominated.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0029_R_d65608047e1372" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nano-Micro Lett">29</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0034_R_d65608047e1372" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0038_R_d65608047e1372" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Rare Met">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the contributions for two processes can be quantified by the equation that <i>i</i> (<i>V</i>) = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> <i>v</i> + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>where i(V): current at a certain voltage; k<sub>1</sub>v: capacitive process; k<sub>2</sub>v<sup>1/2</sup>: diffusion-controlled behavior.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0029_R_d65608047e1399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nano-Micro Lett">29</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0034_R_d65608047e1399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0038_R_d65608047e1399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Rare Met">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The capacitive contribution shows a gradually increased trend from 50.3% at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> to 92.8% at 20&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0003">3g</a>). Furthermore, these values of the FeS/C electrode are &gt;35.3–77.8% of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/C, 42.3-84.8% of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S/C, and 38.3–80.8% of FeS/C@CS (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). Higher capacitive contribution is favorable for faster ion storage and transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0029_R_d65608047e1422" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nano-Micro Lett">29</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0034_R_d65608047e1422" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0038_R_d65608047e1422" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Rare Met">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To further investigate the fast sodium ion transport capability of the FeS/C electrode, its galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information) are tested to obtain D<sub>Na+</sub>, which is 5.6×10<sup>−11</sup>–8.9×10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>. The high D<sub>Na+</sub> benefits the enhancement of sodium ion transport, thus obtaining excellent rate capability.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400246-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0ac8be4c-f2bb-491c-82cc-d54c5608356b/aenm202400246-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0ac8be4c-f2bb-491c-82cc-d54c5608356b/aenm202400246-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3e030d6b-3251-459a-913e-ca48abe603ee/aenm202400246-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0ac8be4c-f2bb-491c-82cc-d54c5608356b/aenm202400246-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400246-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400246" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Half-cell performance characterizations: a) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, b) Cycling performances at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, c) Rate performance, and d) EIS curves of the obtained four electrodes. Kinetic analysis of FeS/C: e) CV curves at different scanning rates, f) Logip versus Logv, and g) pseudocapacitive contribution percentages. Cycling curves h) at 1, i) 2, and j) 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> of FeS/C. k) Performance comparison of reported FeS-based SIB anodes with our FeS/C electrodes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Due to the higher capacity, better cyclability, and more excellent rate performance of FeS/C electrodes, their electrochemical performances are further tested. Besides the first discharge curve, other charge and discharge curves at different cycling numbers at 0.1 C show similar shapes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a</a>, Supporting Information), indicating high reversibility and stability of FeS/C electrode. Compared with the R<sub>ct</sub> of the pristine electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b</a>, Supporting Information), the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> decreases gradually with cycling, which may be ascribed to the electrode activation during cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0029_R_d65608047e1484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nano-Micro Lett">29</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0030_R_d65608047e1484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Mater. Chem. A">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The decrease of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> benefits for ion transport, thus improving rate performances. The cycling stability at high current densities is also tested. Before testing, the electrode is activated in the first three cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. At 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a high capacity of 450.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 87.9% is obtained after 1000 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0003">3h</a>). At 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a high capacity of 416.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 89.8% is obtained after 3000 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0003">3i</a>). Continue to increase current density to 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the FeS/C electrode can endure 10&nbsp;000 cycles at repeated fast ion intercalation and deintercalation with a high capacity of 300.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 81.1% after 10&nbsp;000 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0003">3j</a>). It can be observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0003">3h–j</a> that the CE at high current density is more stable than those at low current density due to the dominant role of capacitive contribution at high current density, which has better reversibility. As seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0005">5j</a> below, the CE is stable at the voltage range dominated by capacitive contribution. In order to further indicate the reason for the improved cycling performance at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the FeS/C electrodes are observed by SEM and TEM after 10&nbsp;000 cycles. Compared with the pristine electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a</a>, Supporting Information), the electrode surface is intact without any cracks (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13b</a>, Supporting Information) and the yolk-shell nanosheet structure is also intact without obvious pulverization (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13c,d</a>, Supporting Information), which demonstrates the high stability of the structure during long cycling. By contrast, the structure of FeS/C nanosheets in the FeS/C@CS electrode after 100 cycles shows obvious destruction due to the lack of hollow space between FeS and C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information), which further indicates the importance of hollow space to maintain the stability of the nanosheet structure. The obtained capacities during long cycling testing at high current densities of our FeS/C electrode are superior to all the previously reported FeS-based SIB anode materials (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0003">3k</a>; Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Significantly, the cycling life over 10&nbsp;000 cycles is unprecedented. Besides, the cycling stability at different mass loading of FeS/C are tested at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). High capacities of 587.5, 508.5, and 432.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> are achieved at mass loadings of 2.5, 4.6, and 6.8&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, respectively, after 100 cycles and corresponding capacity retentions are 93.6, 90.8, and 88.4%. At the high areal mass (6.8&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>), high capacity and retention are obtained, further indicating that the FeS/C electrode has excellent ion transport capability and stability.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202400246-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202400246-sec-0050-title">2.3 Stable and Fast Sodium Ion Storage Mechanism of FeS/C</h3>
            
            <p>To investigate the mechanism that the FeS/C electrode has stable and fast sodium ion storage, in situ TEM, ex situ TEM, ex situ XRD, and In situ magnetometry characterizations are performed. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0004">4a</a> exhibits TEM images before sodiation, which shows a width size of 570.8&nbsp;nm. After sodiation ≈5s, the width size increases to 582.2&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0004">4b</a>), and when ≈11 s, full sodiation is completed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0004">4c</a>; Movie <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) to result in the increase of width size to 593.4&nbsp;nm, corresponding to an ultralow size change of 4.0%. The size of the hollow space reduces from pristine 25.8&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0004">4a</a>) to final 9.7&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0004">4c</a>), suggesting that the hollow structure accommodates the volume expansion of FeS. After full sodiation, the vast nanocrystals (≈3&nbsp;nm, circled by white circles) are formed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0004">4d,e</a>). From SAED pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0004">4f</a>), after sufficient sodiation, single crystal diffraction spots of the original FeS material have transformed into polycrystalline diffraction rings, indicating a significant change in the electrode structure after full sodiation. Through calibration, these diffraction rings are attributed to (200) and (220) crystal planes of Na<sub>2</sub>S (PDF#23-0441) and (110), (200), and (211) crystal planes of Fe (PDF#06-0696), which indicates that the conversion reaction (FeS + 2Na<sup>+</sup> + 2e<sup>−</sup>→ Fe + Na<sub>2</sub>S) occurs to lead to the formation of superparamagnetic Fe nanoparticles. The HAADF image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0004">4g</a>) and its corresponding EDS elemental mapping images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0004">4h–j</a>) show that Fe, S, and C elements are uniformly distributed in the electrode after full sodiation. In order to prove that the conversion reaction still exists and the nanosheet structure is still intact after long-term cycling, the FeS/C electrode, after discharging to 0.01&nbsp;V in the 10&nbsp;000th cycle at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information), is observed by TEM. The nanosheet structure is nearly intact without obvious pulverization after 10&nbsp;000 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a</a>, Supporting Information), in which vast Fe nanocrystals (≈3&nbsp;nm) still exist (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16b,c</a>, Supporting Information) and are uniformly distributed into the nanosheets (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16d–g</a>, Supporting Information). The results fully demonstrate that the FeS/C yolk-shell structure is highly stable, and the conversion reaction is also highly reversible during long-term cycling, which is favorable for stable and fast ion transport upon cycling. It should be noted that the theoretical volume expansion of FeS material after full sodiation is ≈170%.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0011_R_d65608047e1626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Here, in situ TEM shows a low size change of ≈4% in the planar size of the nanosheet, along with an increased size of ≈22.6&nbsp;nm. The size change in the thickness direction must also be considered for the total volume change. We assume that the nanosheet is a cuboid with the same size in length and width. The change of the base area is ≈108.2%, so to achieve a volume change of ≈170%, the corresponding size change in thickness should be ≈157%. The average thickness of the nanosheets is ≈110&nbsp;nm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2i–l</a>, Supporting Information), which subtracts the carbon layer thickness (≈30&nbsp;nm) and the size of hollow space (≈30&nbsp;nm) to indicate a thickness of FeS nanosheet of ≈50&nbsp;nm. When its thickness increases to 78.5&nbsp;nm, its size changes by 157%. During the process, the increased size (≈28.5&nbsp;nm) in thickness is slightly higher than that (≈22.6&nbsp;nm) in planar size but slightly lower than the size of hollow space. Therefore, the obtained hollow structure can alleviate the size changes in the FeS nanosheet's thickness direction to ensure high nanosheet stability during cycling (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400246-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c6039691-7f62-423c-aa63-fca8ea39a3c2/aenm202400246-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c6039691-7f62-423c-aa63-fca8ea39a3c2/aenm202400246-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3a7d1ffd-bc48-4910-aa27-3c55d5d351ca/aenm202400246-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c6039691-7f62-423c-aa63-fca8ea39a3c2/aenm202400246-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400246-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400246" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In situ TEM characterizations of FeS/C electrode: a) TEM image of uncycled electrode; b) TEM image of electrode during sodiation; c–e) TEM images, f) SAED pattern, and g) HAADF and corresponding EDS mapping images of h) Fe, i) S, and j) C of electrode after full sodiation.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The reaction process between FeS and sodium ions is further demonstrated by ex situ XRD, in which D and C represent discharge and charge, respectively. The diffraction peaks of FeS gradually shift toward a low angle when discharge from open circuit voltage to 1&nbsp;V due to intercalation reaction (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0005">5a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). Relative to open circuit voltage and 1.5&nbsp;V, the diffraction peaks of FeS weaken at 1&nbsp;V and disappear at 0.8&nbsp;V, while weak diffraction peaks at ≈38.9° ascribed to (220) crystal plane of Na<sub>2</sub>S appear, indicating that occurrence of the conversion reaction of FeS. From 0.8&nbsp;V (discharge) to 1&nbsp;V (charge), the intensity of diffraction peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>S is basically unchanged, suggesting that Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>S exist within the voltage range. The absence of the diffraction peaks of Fe is attributed to its ultrasmall particle size.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0032_R_d65608047e1679" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When charged to 1.5&nbsp;V, the diffraction peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>S weaken and completely disappear at 3&nbsp;V, while the diffraction peaks of FeS reappear to confirm the reversibility of conversion reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0032_R_d65608047e1686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The sodium ion storage process of FeS/C is further confirmed by CV curves tested at 0.5&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0005">5b</a>). In the first cathodic scanning, an apparent and broad peak at 0.74&nbsp;V, which disappears in subsequent cycles, is ascribed to the formation of SEI, and the intercalation and conversion reaction of FeS.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0011_R_d65608047e1697" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0032_R_d65608047e1697" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, three oxidation peaks at 1.40, 1.85, and 2.16&nbsp;V correspond to the desodiation process to form Na<sub>x</sub>FeS (1.40&nbsp;V) and FeS (1.85 and 2.16&nbsp;V).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0011_R_d65608047e1705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0023_R_d65608047e1705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">23</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0032_R_d65608047e1705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks at 0.10 and 0.08&nbsp;V arise from sodiation and desodiation, respectively, of carbon shell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0030_R_d65608047e1710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Mater. Chem. A">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with the first scan, the positions of reduction peaks change obviously, while the oxidation peaks are basically unchanged in the subsequent scan. The reduction peaks shift to 1.23, 0.86, and 0.24&nbsp;V in the subsequent scan, which are attributed to the intercalation (1.23&nbsp;V) and conversion (0.24 and 0.86&nbsp;V) reactions of FeS.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0011_R_d65608047e1715" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0023_R_d65608047e1715" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">23</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0039_R_d65608047e1715" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nanoscale">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the subsequent CV curves are almost overlapped, further suggesting high reversibility of FeS/C electrode. It should be noted that the&nbsp;position&nbsp;and&nbsp;number&nbsp;of&nbsp;peaks&nbsp;in the CV curves (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0003">3e</a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400246-fig-0005">5b</a>) are different due to the difference in the scan rates. The increase in scanning rate would cause an increase in electrode polarization, leading to an increase in the value difference between the oxidation and reduction potentials. It is manifested that the reduction peaks move toward low voltage and the oxidation peaks move toward high voltage,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0010_R_d65608047e1727" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Eng. J.">10</a>, <a href="#aenm202400246-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400246-bib-0021_R_d65608047e1727" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Eng.
